  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2027184183595987986">Under the reign of Louis XV, corruption and intrigue have been allowed to blossom in France, and Justin Alastair, the notorious Duke of Avon and proud of his soubriquet 'Satanas', flourishes as well. Then, from a dark Parisian back alley, he plucks L-on, a red-headed urchin with strangely familiar looks, just in time for his long over-due schemes of revenge on the Comte de St. Vire. Among the splendours of Versailles and the dignified mansions of England, Justin begins to unfold his sinister plans -- until, that is, Leon becomes the ravishing beauty Leonie ...</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_43035734">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/43035734" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-self-proclaimed-book-ninja" class="left imgcol" title=" Danielle The Book Huntress (Self-Proclaimed Book Ninja)"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1403239075p2/1570669.jpg" alt=" Danielle The Book Huntress (Self-Proclaimed Book Ninja)" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/43035734?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 27, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-self-proclaimed-book-ninja" class="user" title=" Danielle The Book Huntress (Self-Proclaimed Book Ninja)"> Danielle The Book Huntress (Self-Proclaimed Book Ninja)</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/311182.These_Old_Shades" title="Paperback 9780099465829" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Fans of historical romance and fiction</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to  Danielle The Book Huntress (Self-Proclaimed Book Ninja) by: </span>
      AAR Special Titles Listing
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-self-proclaimed-book-ninja?shelf=scary-sexy-cool-dangerous-hero" class="actionLinkLite">scary-sexy-cool-dangerous-hero</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-self-proclaimed-book-ninja?shelf=georgian" class="actionLinkLite">georgian</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-self-proclaimed-book-ninja?shelf=woman-in-pants" class="actionLinkLite">woman-in-pants</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-self-proclaimed-book-ninja?shelf=bad-boy-rake-or-rogue" class="actionLinkLite">bad-boy-rake-or-rogue</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-self-proclaimed-book-ninja?shelf=revenge" class="actionLinkLite">revenge</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-self-proclaimed-book-ninja?shelf=sweet-romance" class="actionLinkLite">sweet-romance</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-self-proclaimed-book-ninja?shelf=fop-dandy" class="actionLinkLite">fop-dandy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-self-proclaimed-book-ninja?shelf=historical-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-self-proclaimed-book-ninja?shelf=older-hero-younger-heroine" class="actionLinkLite">older-hero-younger-heroine</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer43035734">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17909744641513659662">Okay, I am officially a Georgette Heyer reader now. I can see why she is touted as the best of the best when it comes to historical romance. This book was thoroughly enjoyable. I tell you, Avon is a very singular hero. I have read few books with a hero whose dialogue was so expressive, yet ironic at the same time. His wit is so sharp that it could cut diamonds. What's really interesting is that Avon is considered the worst of the worst when it comes to being a debauched rake, yet you never see h</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17909744641513659662">Okay, I am officially a Georgette Heyer reader now. I can see why she is touted as the best of the best when it comes to historical romance. This book was thoroughly enjoyable. I tell you, Avon is a very singular hero. I have read few books with a hero whose dialogue was so expressive, yet ironic at the same time. His wit is so sharp that it could cut diamonds. What's really interesting is that Avon is considered the worst of the worst when it comes to being a debauched rake, yet you never see him looking or acting anything less than elegant. I really admire that Heyer was able to convey this about him without going into his dirty deeds. All that occurs before the book begins. In fact, so much is conveyed and not expressly shown in this story, and done with remarkable skill. I have to say that I read this story, looking to Ms. Heyer to teach me (as a writer) the ability to create powerful dialogue that shows and does not tell. Avon is quite the character. He is definitely a dandy and a fop, wearing bright colors, dripping with lace and jewels, and high-heeled shoes. He even carries a fan that he uses. But he is a man of his time, with a masculinity that is not questionable. And to think we don't have to see him bedhopping to believe in his masculinity!! I thought that Ms. Heyer did a fantastic job in showing Avon's transition from being a cold man with a heart of stone to a loving person. You see this in his manner changing towards friends and family. And you see it in how he interacts with Leonie, who gives him her steadfast, unconditional love from the very beginning.<br /><br /><br /><br />Avon is bent on revenge, but he exemplifies the saying, "Revenge is a dish best served cold." He waited over twenty years to obtain his revenge on his enemy. When the tool of his revenge stumbles into him on a Paris street, it takes a while for you to see how he/she plays into it. Avon concocts a shallow, bored, remote demeanor that is very misleading. The whole while, he is feeling and seeing everything that goes on around him. He sees right through Leon's facade, realizing that she is a girl. Her unique coloring, Red hair and black eyebrows, immediately brings to mind his worst enemy, Saint-Vire. Yet it takes the reader a little while to put the puzzles together. You are not bored though, as the story unfolds and you get to realize what Avon's plan is. For the lines in this story are so laugh out loud funny, you might want to be careful where you break out this book. I'm sure people thought was I was crazy in the moments I read this story in public, because I would burst out into hilarious laughter. As for the revenge plot, you have to read this book until nearly the very end to see how marvelously and skillfully Avon executes his plan for revenge. I have to say, 'Bravo.' And to be honest, it couldn't have happened to a better person. <br /><br /><br />One of my favorite characters in this story is Rupert, Avon's younger brother. Why? Because he made me laugh so hard. He had the best lines. I firmly believe that Loretta Chase must have thought of him when she wrote Bertie in <a title="Lord of Scoundrels (Scoundrels, #3) by Loretta Chase" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/425377.Lord_of_Scoundrels__Scoundrels___3_">Lord of Scoundrels</a>, although Rupert is not nearly as unintelligent as Bertie is portrayed to be. Rupert does a very good job as serving as comic relief in a story that would have been quite dark without these moments of humor. Because of his contribution, I cannot even consider this a dark read. This is also in part to the back and forth dialogue between characters which has the cadence and the humor that endears comedic movies of the 1930s and 40s to this reader and movie buff. The scene with the horse that Rupert 'borrowed' and its livid owner who comes to Avon's home for redress was laugh out loud hilarious. Definitely like a scene from 1940s slapstick comedy at its best. Some of the characters that add to the wonderful atmosphere are Fanny, Avon's sister, Marling, her staid husband, and Hugh, Avon's less staid, but certainly moral friend, who often disproves of Avon's behavior, but is a steadfast friend all the same.<br /><br />Leonie is a character that I liked, although at times her ingenue nature was a bit much for me. The older I get, the less I really enjoy the very young, vivacious, extremely audacious-mannered heroines. I did not let that lessen my enjoyment of this story, for Leonie is the perfect foil for Avon. This older, very jaded hero needed a very young, sweet heroine with a zest for life. He would not have fallen in love and committed to a happy ending as a happily married man otherwise. In fact, I think his cold heart would have grown colder through the years, probably pushing everyone away who loved him, had it not been for Leonie's advent into his life. <br /><br />Leonie is the character that everyone loves. I suppose she might be considered a 'Mary Sue' by some, but again, I don't quibble, for this story needed a character like her for it be successful. Also I reject the notion that an old fashioned, feel-good story doesn't have its place in the world. They most certainly do. And at the end of the day, the escapades of this hoyden do make you smile and feel good.<br /><br />This novel gave me a very good look into 18th century life in France and England, for which I was grateful. It is said that Heyer's book stand up against the most stringent historical accuracy sticklers. She is a testiment to the genre of historical romance, which is always taking hits as being low-brow fiction. I wonder why this has not been made into film, for I feel it would make a wonderful movie. And it has an appeal outside of those readers who enjoy romance.<br /><br />This book was a joy to read, and it has made me an eager fan of Heyer. I would love to read more of her books, and since I've heard that she had some older, sensible heroine (one of my favorite types in historical romance), I expect to enjoy those books just as much, if not more.<br /><br />For those romance fans who haven't read Heyer, take it from me. You really should give her a try. I don't think you'll be disappointed.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17909744641513659662">...more</a>

              <a href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=squeaky-clean-romance" class="actionLinkLite">squeaky-clean-romance</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=suspense" class="actionLinkLite">suspense</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=georgian" class="actionLinkLite">georgian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer583449439">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16509017997509886614"><em>"La vengeance est un mets que l'on doit manger froid."</em> (Revenge is a dish that should be eaten cold.)<br /><br />--Credited to Charles Talleyrand, French diplomat (1754–1838).<br /><br />Revenge and a Cinderella-type plot make a great combination in <em>These Old Shades,</em> one of Georgette Heyer's more well-known historical novels, set in the later 1700's. <br /><br /><img width="214" height="162" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1426912459i/14125209.jpg" /><br /><br />The half-French Duke of Avon is walking down the street when a small body hurtles into him. He looks into the face of this young urchin and suddenly sees the potential for</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16509017997509886614"><em>"La vengeance est un mets que l'on doit manger froid."</em> (Revenge is a dish that should be eaten cold.)<br /><br />--Credited to Charles Talleyrand, French diplomat (1754–1838).<br /><br />Revenge and a Cinderella-type plot make a great combination in <em>These Old Shades,</em> one of Georgette Heyer's more well-known historical novels, set in the later 1700's. <br /><br /><img width="214" height="162" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1426912459i/14125209.jpg" /><br /><br />The half-French Duke of Avon is walking down the street when a small body hurtles into him. He looks into the face of this young urchin and suddenly sees the potential for taking revenge on the man who has been his mortal enemy for twenty years. So he buys the curly red-headed young orphan off the boy's loutish older brother, takes him home and cleans him up, and presents him to Paris society as his new page. <em>*Some limited spoilers follow*</em><br /><br />It soon turns out that this boy is not a boy after all, but an impish 19 year old girl. So Léon becomes Léonie, as the Duke takes her to his sister in England, charging her to teach Léonie manners and train her to act as a young girl in high society should. <br /><br /><img width="204" height="176" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1394401321i/8848437.jpg" /><br /><br />Various shenanigans ensue, as Léonie resists fitting herself into the mold society expects, and the Comte Saint-Vire, the Duke's old enemy, takes some drastic and potentially lethal steps to try to avoid the noose he can see headed his way.<br /><br />I've been tempted to knock this book down a star or two for a couple of things in the plot that really irk me: The twenty year age difference between the Duke and Léonie (he calls her "infant," for heaven's sake), which everyone in his family handwaves because they think the two fit so well together. The insistence that "blood will out": everyone can tell Léonie is well-born because of her looks and natural grace, while a boy born to peasants but raised in high society still looks and acts boorish and wants nothing so much as to leave Paris and live on a farm.<br /><br />But in the end, this book was just so much darn fun to read that I have to award all the stars. Léonie is small and passionate and absolutely hilarious, given to calling people names and flying off the handle when they anger her: "He is a pig-person. I hate him. I am glad that I bit him, and threw the coffee over him."<br /><br />Justin, the jaded Duke of Avon, is one of those mastermind type of characters I adore, weaving his revenge plot while everyone else around him tries to figure out what his intentions are and why he's doing what he is. And yet the suave Duke gets tripped up by this irreverent young girl he's taken in, who sees him clearly but adores him nevertheless. He acts languid and casual around others, but every once in a while you see his true determination and strength of character show through his posing. He's also snarky and sarcastic and has a dry sense of humor:<blockquote>"M'sieur, you will think I come upon a strange errand, but I have a wife!" [De Faugenac] beamed at Avon, and nodded several times.<br /><br />"I felicitate you, m'sieur," said Avon gravely.<br /><br />"Yes, yes! A wife! It will explain all."<br /><br />"It always does," answered his Grace. . . .<br /><br />"She eats her heart out for your so lovely, your so enchanting, your so elegant--"<br /><br />Avon held up his hand. "M'sieur, my policy has ever been to eschew married women."<br /><br />"But -- but -- what do you mean, m'sieur? My wife pines for your page. . . She cannot sleep a night until she knows that he is hers."<br /><br />"It seems that madame is destined to spend many sleepless nights," said Avon.</blockquote>The humorous dialogue, mixed with a suspenseful story of revenge and the cat and mouse game between the Duke of Avon and his enemy, the Comte Saint-Vire, was just a pleasure to read.<br /><br />Interesting note: This book is a semi-sequel to Heyer's first novel, <a title="The Black Moth by Georgette Heyer" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/311322.The_Black_Moth">The Black Moth</a> (which is old enough that you can get it for free on Gutenberg and other sites). Apparently Heyer wanted to do a sequel but the characters didn't quite fit what she wanted to do in this book. So she renamed them and changed some of their characteristics and backstory, so that the new characters are "shades" of the old ones. Hence the name of this novel, which came from a Victorian-era poem that appears at the front of this book in some editions. The poem reads in part:<br /><br /><em>Whereas with these old shades of mine, <br />Their ways and dress delight me; <br />And should I trip by word or line, <br />They cannot well indict me.</em><blockquote>--Austin Dobson: Epilogue to Eighteenth Century Vignettes</blockquote>All the stars!</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16509017997509886614">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_610227110">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/610227110" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4529669-willow" class="left imgcol" title="Willow "><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1447206325p2/4529669.jpg" alt="Willow " /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/610227110?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 13, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4529669-willow" class="user" title="Willow ">Willow </a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/6807878-these-old-shades" title="Paperback 9781402219474" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/4529669-willow?shelf=angels" class="actionLinkLite">angels</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4529669-willow?shelf=18th-century" class="actionLinkLite">18th-century</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer610227110">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12477421838768533">I enjoyed <em>These Old Shades.</em> I think Heyer writes vivid, opinionated characters and she captures the Georgian period beautifully. I must admit though, I’m not sure this book is for everybody. There’s a lot of yakking. These characters talk, talk, and even more talk. Heyer doesn’t directly tell you what her characters are up to either, so you have to get to know them by what they say. Some of the dialogue is pretty hilarious, and some of it is kind of repetitive. To me this makes the characters mo</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12477421838768533">I enjoyed <em>These Old Shades.</em> I think Heyer writes vivid, opinionated characters and she captures the Georgian period beautifully. I must admit though, I’m not sure this book is for everybody. There’s a lot of yakking. These characters talk, talk, and even more talk. Heyer doesn’t directly tell you what her characters are up to either, so you have to get to know them by what they say. Some of the dialogue is pretty hilarious, and some of it is kind of repetitive. To me this makes the characters more visceral, but it can also slow down the action. You definitely get to know the two lovers. <br /><br />I thought the Duke was a hoot. He has somewhat questionable morals, and in truth he starts out as the villain of the story. He’s carrying around a twenty year old grudge that he simply MUST get even for. He’s very clever though and amusing, and let’s not forget smooth, so you can’t help but root for him. I have to admit, in the beginning he sort of gave me the impression of being a perv with the way he takes young Leon under his wing, but then you find out he has a whole different agenda. <br /><br />Of course, some of my more skeptical impressions of the Duke, may be due to the fact that Leonie was so naïve and worshiping, I thought she was a child. Of course later on you find out she kept a lot of things to herself. When she feels comfortable, a lot more personality comes to the surface and she gets a lot more lippy. Lol<br /><br />After I finished this I realized that the Duke and Leonie are polar opposites. Where she is trusting, he is cynical. Where she is impulsive, the Duke is calculating. She is innocent, and the Duke is not. They truly do compliment each other. It’s no wonder they fall in love.<br /><br />Of course not all was peachy. There were some eye rolling moments, like Heyer’s snobby assertion that being noble and refined is something you’re born with and will show even if you are raised by a pig farmer. Thus it cannot be learned (which I think is a bunch of hooey). But I’m sure there are some blue blood nobles out there who’d disagree. Yet this antiquated view actually fits perfectly with the period and gives a true aristocratic vibe, which in many ways is preferable to the politically correct views that seem to infiltrate historical fiction now. <br /><br />So for my review, I give ****1/2. I thought this book was a lot of fun. I will forever think of the Duke as a young, smooth talking Peter O’Toole and Leonie as Milla Jovovich from <em>The Fifth Element</em>. lol<br />Speaking of movies, how come they've never made a book or miniseries from one of Heyer's books. I think her books would be perfect for the Jane Austin crowd. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12477421838768533">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <a href="/user/show/94602-kelly" class="left imgcol" title="Kelly"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1437154881p2/94602.jpg" alt="Kelly" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1678307?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 01, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/94602-kelly" class="user" title="Kelly">Kelly</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/311182.These_Old_Shades" title="Paperback 9780099465829" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">regency or georgian lit fans</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=brit-lit" class="actionLinkLite">brit-lit</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=romantical" class="actionLinkLite">romantical</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=owned" class="actionLinkLite">owned</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=partialprejudicedandignorantopinion" class="actionLinkLite">partialprejudicedandignorantopinion</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=shes-quite-an-original-my-dear" class="actionLinkLite">shes-quite-an-original-my-dear</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1678307">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer517395080969018690">This is one of my favorite romance novels, ever. I am not even afraid to review it here on this pretentious book site, I love it so much. I have worn through my copy of it. It is lovely beat up and bent back to the pages of all my favorite parts. The Heyer hallmarks are all here: great characters, hilarious plot, breaking the genre rules, a somewhat effete hero (but in a manly way). The main character, Leonie, is absolutely adorable. I don't even care if others find her annoying, she makes me la</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText517395080969018690">This is one of my favorite romance novels, ever. I am not even afraid to review it here on this pretentious book site, I love it so much. I have worn through my copy of it. It is lovely beat up and bent back to the pages of all my favorite parts. The Heyer hallmarks are all here: great characters, hilarious plot, breaking the genre rules, a somewhat effete hero (but in a manly way). The main character, Leonie, is absolutely adorable. I don't even care if others find her annoying, she makes me laugh. I've been reading this book since I was 13 and I still feel that way.<br /><br />This book actually has some great layers. There's real darkness and fear and complexity in the characters. It isn't just the farce that it could be. Far from it. There's real mystery and a deep sympathy to the hero, Justin. Easily the first book hero I ever fell in love with. He's quite gothic, but with that Regency sprinkling of humor that I love. (Think Mr. Rochester... but polished. And hilarious.)<br /><br />Whatever, shut up. I love Georgette Heyer. This was one of the books that really helped along my love for 18th/19th century Brit lit.</span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/18780551-anne?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/18780551-anne?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/18780551-anne?shelf=romance" class="actionLinkLite">romance</a>,

              <a href="/review/list/18780551-anne?shelf=young-heroines" class="actionLinkLite">young-heroines</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/18780551-anne?shelf=georgette-heyer" class="actionLinkLite">georgette-heyer</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/18780551-anne?shelf=laugh-out-loud-funny" class="actionLinkLite">laugh-out-loud-funny</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/18780551-anne?shelf=rakes" class="actionLinkLite">rakes</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/18780551-anne?shelf=swashbucklers" class="actionLinkLite">swashbucklers</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/18780551-anne?shelf=dukes" class="actionLinkLite">dukes</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/18780551-anne?shelf=grab-during-fire" class="actionLinkLite">grab-during-fire</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/18780551-anne?shelf=older-heroes" class="actionLinkLite">older-heroes</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/18780551-anne?shelf=dear-sir-i-am-in-love-with-you" class="actionLinkLite">dear-sir-i-am-in-love-with-you</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/18780551-anne?shelf=unconventional-and-proud-of-it" class="actionLinkLite">unconventional-and-proud-of-it</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/18780551-anne?shelf=best-of-2013" class="actionLinkLite">best-of-2013</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/18780551-anne?shelf=swoony-heroes" class="actionLinkLite">swoony-heroes</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer596363218">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14963767375127811395">Update: I have it on good authority that this is an accurate representation of me while thinking of this book (or should I specify, the hero!):<br /><img class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1394401321ra/8848436.gif" /><br /><br /><br />By the way this book only gets better and funnier with each re-read I do! ;) Love these crazy people forever &lt;3 <br /><br /><br />{Please note that the following <s>essay</s> review contains spoilers}<br /><br />I'm not sure that I can entirely express how much I truly love this book and how absolutely amazing it is.<br />I remember reading <em>The Black Moth</em> by the same author a while ago, and sec</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14963767375127811395">Update: I have it on good authority that this is an accurate representation of me while thinking of this book (or should I specify, the hero!):<br /><img class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1394401321ra/8848436.gif" /><br /><br /><br />By the way this book only gets better and funnier with each re-read I do! ;) Love these crazy people forever &lt;3 <br /><br /><br />{Please note that the following <s>essay</s> review contains spoilers}<br /><br />I'm not sure that I can entirely express how much I truly love this book and how absolutely amazing it is.<br />I remember reading <em>The Black Moth</em> by the same author a while ago, and secretly having a shameful preference for the book's villain, the Duke of Andover, over the main protagonist. And when I read <em>The Convenient Marriage</em>, Lord Lethbridge was also my favourite character, against my better judgement. I don't know what it is exactly that make me love those sarcastic, cynical, mocking, badass villains so much, but I had always hoped that Andover could have had a well-developed story of his own and a happy ending. Perhaps it was the idea of potential redemption that make me like the above mentioned villains, I don't know.<br /><br />Nevertheless, when I found out that <em>These Old Shades</em> was a sort of sequel to <em>The Black Moth</em>, I got super excited and knew that I had to read it. I got even MORE excited when I found out that Heyer had used the same characters as in <em>The Black Moth</em>, but had simply changed their names (hence the title <em>
  <u>These Old Shades</u>
</em>, as she made the characters "shades" of their former selves). Then I got definitely over-the-top excited when I found out that Andover was going to be the main hero of the story, under the name of Justin Alastair "Satanas" His Grace the Duke of Avon.<br /><br />Yes, all those names indeed represent the same person. As everyone who has read this novel is aware, deciphering who is whom at the beginning of the story is slightly confusing, since Heyer uses many ways to name the Duke in just a few pages (I actually thought there was about 4 different guys in that scene when Avon is talking with Davenant, just after he brought Léon to the house). But once you've figured that Avon is referred to by about 15 different names, it becomes way easier.<br /><br />One of the things I simply love about Georgette Heyer's books is that she doesn't take 10 chapters to just "introduce" what is going to happen, or who the characters are. She starts off immediately with something interesting, that grasps the reader's attention right away. In this case, the Duke of Avon buys a boy he finds in the street, who is fleeing from his brother's beating hand. I was definitely intrigued, as we see that Avon has a motive for his actions which he refuses to share.<br /><br />By reading a lot of reviews here on Goodreads about this book, I found that the two major problems that most people encountered with <em>These Old Shades</em> was that the two protagonists were annoying (particularly Léonie) and that the love story was very ick-creepy-pedophile-and-just-plain-disgusting. My modern self understands these points of view, but my inner Georgian lady thinks the hero and heroine awesome and their love story beautiful.<br /><br />The character of Avon is indeed very particular. Picking up from when <em>The Black Moth</em> finishes, we find a Duke (around 40 years of age) carrying a terrible reputation as a debauched rake, one of his most recent scandal having been to kidnap the woman with whom he was in love (some reviewers complained that that subject was not alluded to further in the book, but that whole "abduction" thing is actually part of the main plot in <em>The Black Moth</em>). We see none, however, of his bad deeds in <em>These Old Shades</em>, but we <em>know</em> that he's bad just by the way he speaks, and through his relationships with his family and friends. He is as deadly with a sword as he is with words. People fear him and distrust him in general. I really enjoyed the fact the Heyer did not have to include scenes where he acted wrongly to make me believe that he was a devil (all that was actually told in <em>The Black Moth</em>). And another thing that I found particularly interesting was that the Duke was a tall and handsome man, very much masculine and viril, and yet he was described as wearing a powdered wig (!), lace, velvet, gold, emeralds and I- don't-know- what-that-I-never-imagined-men-wore (!!), carrying a <strong>fan</strong> (!!!!) and <strong>mincing</strong> around on high heels (!!!!!!!!!!!!). Yes, I can see how some had problems with that. As a Georgian era lover, it didn't bother me that much (I actually thought the fan-involving parts to be absolutely hilarious!).<br /><br /><strong>
  <em>"The Duke fanned himself with an air, handling the chicken-skin like a woman.^<br />[..]<br />His Grace smiled slowly, eyes nearly shut.<br />'The fan expresses my emotions. I must kiss March's hands and feet. Metaphorically speaking.' He waved the fan gently to and fro.<br />Davenant glared at him for a moment, annoyed at his nonchalance. <br />[...]<br />'Justin, pray put that fan away!'"</em>
</strong><br /><br />I could quote this scene on and on, as it is one of my favourites (I mean, poor Davenant is trying to have a serious conversation with Justin and he is just fooling around, fanning himself! lol!). I personally loved every scene in which the Duke had a say, and to me, he is an amazing character. But moving on. I could talk about him all day long, so let's move on.<br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1393799260i/8765190.jpg" /><br /><br />Léonie is one of my favourite young heroines. She's just adorable. Yes, I admit that she did seem to act a lot younger than her given age (19), what with talking nonsense half the time, being so bloodthirsty and worshipping the Duke like a god. And yes, from a certain point of view, I can see that the relationship between Avon and Léonie is made a little creepy/disturbing, given that there are 20+ years that separate them, and that Avon spends the better part of the book referring to her as "my infant", "my child", etc...They seem to have more of a father-daughter relationship than a an actual romantic one. <br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1394401321i/8848437.jpg" /><br /><br />However, there is another way of looking at it. <br />Just think about it for a minute.<br />The Duke of Avon is a cold-hearted man who has never truly cared or loved anyone or anything, and who has never been loved. (OK, there are perhaps a few exeptions...Hugh Davenant is his friend and it is said that he once loved Jennifer Beauchamp, but he tried to abduct her - twice! So...one sees the point). Then, one day, Léonie comes into his life in a most unexpected way, and gifts him with an unconditional and undying adoration. Léonie has known only misery in the nineteen years she has lived. The Duke one day decides to keep her. He shows himself to be unexpectedly good and kind to her. Isn't is normal then, for Léonie, who has never been properly cared for, and who has a terrible past, to come to love the man who got her out of her misery? He may not have acted from charity at first, but Avon's character change is one of the best aspects of this novel.<br />He starts as a Satan figure, and becomes a godly one (at least, to Léonie).<br />In a way, each rescued the other. The Duke saved Léonie from a terrible life of lies and agony, and Léonie saved the Duke from his sinful rakish lifestyle.<br /><br />Honestly, I cannot blame Léonie for acting the way she does. In a world where all women are supposed to be the same, wearing giant skirts, curtseying, having their hands kissed, being accomplished, playing a lower role in the society, etc...it is no wonder that with the kind of past she's had, she chose to be different. And everyone loves her because of that. Women love her because she's sweet, beautiful, unique and funny. Men love her because she's different, espiègle and enjouée.<br />And...Avon comes to love her because she's herself. She's the only one who has ever dared to contradict or to talk back to him, and he likes it. It went from:<br /><br /><strong>
  <em>" 'If it is your will that I obey this Walker,’ said Léon with dignity, ‘I will do it y-your-r-r- Gr-r-race!’<br />Justin looked him over.<br />‘Certainly you will do so. And I prefer that you call me Monseigneur.’<br />[…]<br />‘This Walker has told me that when I speak to you, Monseigneur, I must say “your-r-r” ah, bah! I cannot, enfin!’<br />For one moment Justin stared haughtily at his page. <br />[…]<br />‘Be very careful,’ Justin warned him.”</em>
</strong><br /><br />to:<br /><br /><strong>
  <em>” ‘I called to Rupert as loud as I could and kicked the pig-person-‘<br /> ‘The Comte de Saint-Vire.’<br /> ‘Yes, the pig-person- on his legs a great many times. He did not like it at all.’<br /> ‘That,’ said his Grace, ‘does not altogether surprise me.’<br />[…]<br /> ‘Oh, you know the rest, Monseigneur! He gave an evil drink – pig-wash! He called it coffee.’<br /> ‘Then, let us also call it coffee, child, I beg of you. I can support “pig-person” but “pig-wash” I will not endure.’<br /> ‘But it was, Monseigneur! I threw it at him and he swore.’<br />[…]<br /> ‘Poor infant!’ His Grace tweaked one curl. ‘But a most indomitable infant withal.’”</em>
</strong><br /><br />Ahem – once again, I have to restrain myself from quoting the whole book!<br />Another awesome point about Heyer’s characterization is that she makes her secondary characters as fiery and fun as her primary ones. Justin’s younger siblings, Lady Fanny Marling and Lord Rupert Alastair, are no exception to this rule. Every scene involving them was super funny (especially with Rupert). What was the most fun about them, though, was their realization that the Duke was falling in love with Léonie. Fanny’s “plans” for them to get married and Rupert’s rebuttals that “Justin-is-too-old-and-too-devilish-for-her-and-besides-he-will-never-marry-a-bastard” were simply hilarious!<br /><br />It also showed that their relationship as a family was evolving. In the beginning, Justin’s relationship with Fanny and Rupert is lacking trust, respect, and love. They fear their brother and he doesn’t care about them. As we come to see a softer side to Avon, however, we can see how that relationship gradually changes.<br /><br />When Avon meets Rupert for the first time, (in the book) when he is wounded in an inn on a roadside in rural France, he acts towards him with none of his usual biting sarcasm, and even takes care of him. Rupert is greatly surprised that he is “basking in the sunshine of his approuval”, as Avon seems really proud of his courage to have saved Léonie when she was kidnapped, and does not know at first to what he should attribute the change. <br /><br />Rupert and Léonie, on the other hand, have a brother/sister relationship from the first, and act like veritable youngsters when they are together; calling each other names, and fighting in the woods. Rupert is a lot closer in age with Léonie than Avon is, and it is a few times suggested that she should get with him as a couple instead of focusing on the Duke, but that Léonie can absolutely not do, and she treats Rupert as her brother throughout the novel. Avon yet plays again a sort of fatherly figure to them, recommending that they stop calling each other “imbéciles” and gently scolding Léonie for her unladylike behaviour. In a way, playing the “reprimanding older brother”, Justin gets closer to Rupert, as he probably never acted in such a way that shows that he cares at least a bit about him. <br /><br />One of my absolute favourite scenes that clearly illustrates the change his Grace has with Rupert and Fanny is the sort of family-dinner they have with a few friends, on the eve before Léonie is to curtsy to the Polite World. Seeing them all talk gaily, amiably and at perfect ease was very touching.<br /><br /><strong>
  <em>” ‘I believe I have several times requested you not to call Rupert “imbecile”, infant.’<br /> ‘But, Monseigneur, he is an imbécile!’ she protested. ‘You know he is!’<br /> ‘Undoubtedly, ma fille, but I do not tell the whole world so.’<br /> ‘Then I don’t know what I am to call him, ‘ said Léonie. ‘He calls me spitfire, Monseigneur, and wild-cat.’<br /> ‘And so she is, by Gad!’ exclaimed his lordship.<br /> ‘I am not, Rupert. I am a lady. Monseigneur says so.’<br /> ‘A manifestly false assertion,’ said his Grace. ’But I cannot remember ever having said anything of the kind, infant.<br />[…]<br /> ‘But, Monseigneur, you said only a minute ago that your memory is not at all good.’<br />There was a shout of laughter; Avon’s own eyes were alight with it. He picked up his fan and dealt Léonie a rap across the knuckles.”</em>
</strong><br /><br />Haha, yes, I know, I know, that fan again! But I just had to include it in the passage!<br /><br />Avon’s improving relationship with his sister is really demonstrated in the following scenes, as they work together to present Léonie to the respectable people at her first ball, and to make sure that she enjoys herself. Fanny is constantly looking for Justin to ask him which lady or lord Léonie should be introduced to next. As much as the Duke plays a sort of protector/father figure to Léonie, I believe that Fanny plays a very maternal role for Léonie. She treats her like her daughter (and even says so herself), educating her and dressing her with her own gowns. At first she thought Léonie to be a real trial, but by the end she loved taking care of her.<br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1394401321i/8848438.jpg" /><br /><br />The one thing that disappointed me about this first ball scene was that Léonie and Avon did not even dance together! I was secretly really hoping for it, especially since he had promised her that he would dance with her in a previous conversation.<br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1394401321i/8848439._SX540_.jpg" /><br /><br />Oh well, that was really the only minor detail that disappointed me (oh, and of course the fact that the book cover completely reveals a major spoiler!!!!!).<br /><br />As much I as enjoyed TOS though, I wouldn’t recommend it to everyone. Not everyone can stand all the “my dears” and “my infants”, and the jewels, and lace, and gold, and powder and hand-kissing and Georgian slang (the book’s literally full of it!). It’s not for everyone. However, for those like me who are totally obsessed with those kind of things, <em>These Old Shades</em> is the perfect book. In fact, it’s worth the read just for the over-the-top witty conversations.<br /><br />And as for the love story…if at times I felt that Avon overused the nickname “my infant” and I wasn’t sure if he only loved Léonie as his ward, the ending definitely removed all doubts and left me assured and satisfied that they were both madly in love with each other.<br /><br />The re-telling of Léonie’s tragic story by the Duke is perhaps the most touching part of this novel (and, yes, yes one of my favourites!). As he told her tale, his love for her shone through his voice at certain passages, and the answering adoration she held for him was echoed through the acute sacrifice she made to save his reputation <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[ she fled his house for fear she would expose him to a scandal when she discovered that Saint-Vire was her father and that she was probably his bastard-daughter <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />When Avon rescued Léonie and later decided to keep her under his watchful eyes, his motives were strictly selfish. He had his own reasons for keeping her, and hoped to use her for his own ends. However, these motives soon took an important turn as he discovered Léonie’s unique personality, and came to love what he saw. His desire for revenge was then intensified, perhaps, but had a very different meaning.<br /><br />When the Duke confesses his feelings for Léonie, he openly admits his unworthiness by implying that she is his superior and that he does not deserve her, or rather, that she does not deserve a man with such a tainted past. By thus humiliating himself in front of the woman he loves, it undoubtedly proves that he is an altogether reformed character. <br /><br /><strong>
  <em>” His Grace looked deep into her eyes, and then went down on one knee, and raised her hand to his lips.<br />‘Little one,’ he said, very low, ‘since you will stoop to wed me, I pledge you my word that you shall not in the future have cause to regret it.’”</em>
</strong><br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1394401321i/8848440.jpg" /><br /><br />Sigh…Here is my Duke of Andover from <em>The Black Moth</em> an entirely amended rake.<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[But boy, did he get his full revenge on Saint-Vire! Wow! Not only does he make him live hell by waiting so long to strike his final blow, and when he does it, proceeds with such intelligence and wit, telling Léonie’s story in front of everyone and causing Mme de Saint-Vire to have a total breakdown, but he ends up MARRYING SAINT-VIRE’S DAUGHTER, when the point of Avon’s revenge was not only to avenge Léonie, but also to get back to him for what had happened between them 20 years before when Saint-Vire would not let Avon MARRY HIS SISTER! AND NOW AVON MARRIES HIS DAUGHTER, HA! <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />This book is genius. Pure genius.<br /><br />And just beautiful, wonderful, awesome, amazing, incredible.<br /><br />Now, the question is, what the heck am I supposed to do with my life???!?!?<br /><br />Well, thank God for <em>Devil’s Cub</em>!!!!!!!!! <br /><br /><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--></span>

      <a href="/user/show/10571559-hana" class="user" title="Hana">Hana</a>

        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1219004305">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17351171471489230999">The literary equivalent of those marvelous British period dramas with utterly glorious settings and luscious costumes.<br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1426258984i/13993974._SY540_.jpg" /><br /><br />I can never resist this sort thing. Pages of details of soft white brocades, ruffle upon ruffle, falling over great hoops, auburn hair piled in elaborate curls, pearls against soft pale skin, and perfect accessories like...chicken-skin fans. <br /><br />And since this is 18th century France before the Revolution, the men are just as gorgeous and even more colorful....<br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1426258984i/13993975._SY540_.jpg" /><br /><br />When we first meet the h</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17351171471489230999">The literary equivalent of those marvelous British period dramas with utterly glorious settings and luscious costumes.<br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1426258984i/13993974._SY540_.jpg" /><br /><br />I can never resist this sort thing. Pages of details of soft white brocades, ruffle upon ruffle, falling over great hoops, auburn hair piled in elaborate curls, pearls against soft pale skin, and perfect accessories like...chicken-skin fans. <br /><br />And since this is 18th century France before the Revolution, the men are just as gorgeous and even more colorful....<br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1426258984i/13993975._SY540_.jpg" /><br /><br />When we first meet the hero, the Duke of Avon, he is arrayed "in a full-skirted coat of purple satin, heavily laced with gold," his wig immaculately powdered, carrying a beribboned cane, and mincing along in high heels. The Duke is returning to his palatial Paris hôtel from a night of gaming or perhaps other amusements when an urchin stumbles across his path and begs for help: "Great violet-blue eyes gazed wildly up at him, terror in their depths." <br /><br />This is a Georgette Heyer romance so we all know where this is going, but getting there is splendid fun. The plot is a lively but improbable mix of The Count of Monte Cristo and My Fair Lady yet it's all so glittering that I hardly noticed the plot holes as I gawked at ballroom scenes at Versailles and yearned to own one marvelous gown after another. <br /><br />The descriptions are splendid, the dialog witty, and the protagonists are likable, particularly the little urchin who is transformed into....well that <em>would</em> be telling! <br /><br />Along the way, we get to meet luminaries at the court of Louis V, including the famed courtesan, Madam Pompadour....<br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1426261143i/13994292._SY540_.jpg" /><br /><br />splendidly dressed, of course! <br /><br />I've read most of Georgette Heyer's Regency romances, but I'm new to those set in 18th century France and England. You can be sure I'll be back for more! Read with fellow <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/2234572-these-old-shades-march-2015-chapters-1-18">Georgette Heyer Fans</a> who added so much to the pleasure--thank you all! </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17351171471489230999">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <a href="/review/show/1910356?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 26, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/113980-trin" class="user" title="Trin">Trin</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/311182.These_Old_Shades" title="Paperback 9780099465829" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/113980-trin?shelf=historicalfiction" class="actionLinkLite">historicalfiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/113980-trin?shelf=romance" class="actionLinkLite">romance</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/113980-trin?shelf=english-lit" class="actionLinkLite">english-lit</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/113980-trin?shelf=genderfuck" class="actionLinkLite">genderfuck</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1910356">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14786470188609184384">Most fandom people I've talked to either love or at least rather like this book, but it seems it's my turn to feel all "bwah?" and left out, as Punk does with <a title=" The Dreyfus Affair" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=%20The%20Dreyfus%20Affair"> The Dreyfus Affair</a> and Siria does with <a title=" Swordspoint" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=%20Swordspoint"> Swordspoint</a>. I hated it. I despised pretty much all the characters, other than Hugh and Rupert—Leonie was irritating, and Avon was just creepy. I know he was supposed to be "Satanas"—the devil of a man who isn't really that bad, but I found him neither enjoyably naughty nor charming; he was just kin</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14786470188609184384">Most fandom people I've talked to either love or at least rather like this book, but it seems it's my turn to feel all "bwah?" and left out, as Punk does with <a title=" The Dreyfus Affair" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=%20The%20Dreyfus%20Affair"> The Dreyfus Affair</a> and Siria does with <a title=" Swordspoint" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=%20Swordspoint"> Swordspoint</a>. I hated it. I despised pretty much all the characters, other than Hugh and Rupert—Leonie was irritating, and Avon was just creepy. I know he was supposed to be "Satanas"—the devil of a man who isn't really that bad, but I found him neither enjoyably naughty nor charming; he was just kind of slimy. The idea of him and Leonie being together really skeeved me out, not because of the age difference—I actually <em>like</em> an age difference, when it's done well—but because of the power dynamic, I guess. All the power was Avon's, both practically and emotionally, and throughout the whole book Leonie was worshipful of him and he condescending towards her. Ew. I also didn't see the slash at all; Hugh was one of the few nice characters, as I said, so I guess it could be construed that he put up with Avon because he was in love with him, but Avon didn't seem particularly gay to me—he was just an 18th Century dude who lived in France and was a bit of a vain ass. The overall package was not appealing, and neither was this book, which is too bad, because I really enjoyed the only other Heyer I've read, <a title=" The Masqueraders" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=%20The%20Masqueraders"> The Masqueraders</a>.<br /><br />Before I read <em>These Old Shades</em>, I was planning to read <a title=" The Grand Sophy" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=%20The%20Grand%20Sophy"> The Grand Sophy</a> soon, but now I'm not so sure; <em>Shades</em> turned me off, and I also heard that <em>Sophy</em> has a really ugly Jewish stereotype in it. Those of you who've read it: what do you think?</span>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <a href="/user/show/1010492-amy-s" class="user" title="Amy S">Amy S</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer852183714">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10322779296385648130">While I am not a tremendous Heyer fan, I do enjoy knocking them back like m&amp;ms. That probably doesn't make sense to anyone but me. So by that I mean, like m&amp;ms they don't have a lot of substance or weight, and it really annoys me that I keep eating them, and yet I can't seem to stop.<br /><br />My understanding is that this is one of her first novels. It concerns Justin, the Duke of Avon, and Leone who -- whoopsie -- turns out to be Leonie. The Duke of Avon is supposed to be a first class rake of lo</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10322779296385648130">While I am not a tremendous Heyer fan, I do enjoy knocking them back like m&amp;ms. That probably doesn't make sense to anyone but me. So by that I mean, like m&amp;ms they don't have a lot of substance or weight, and it really annoys me that I keep eating them, and yet I can't seem to stop.<br /><br />My understanding is that this is one of her first novels. It concerns Justin, the Duke of Avon, and Leone who -- whoopsie -- turns out to be Leonie. The Duke of Avon is supposed to be a first class rake of low reputation, nicknamed "Satanas." However, we never quite learn what he has done to deserve this nickname, and because of that it was hard for me to believe he was really that bad. He is always impeccably dressed and mannered, and I cannot tell you how much I enjoyed his razor-sharp wit. No one can so elegantly put someone in their place like the Duke. <br /><br />Leonie is young, young, young, rough around the edges as she's been raised a boy, and quite the ingenue, which was annoying at times, but her sincerity and earnestness saved the day. <br /><br />The other characters in the book were quite a bit of fun as well: Justin's sister Fanny was a riot, and Rupert his brother the comic relief. Although with that it bugged me a bit as I am no fan of Heyer's slang. <br /><br />Speaking of slang, the best thing about this book is that the word "odious" did not appear once.<br /><br />This is a book where I truly was unsure of how it would end. I was not sure the hero/heroine would be together or if they should be. I enjoyed all the twists and turns, even though a bit of the book dragged for me somewhere in the middle. But as I have no problem skipping through sections of a book, it didn't hamper me too much. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10322779296385648130">...more</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1170416409">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7073755410117954227">The book is meant to be a light hearted, entertaining affair. It suited me perfectly for the holiday season. Avon's witty dialogue was always hilarious. Though I guessed the mystery surrounding Leon's birth from the very beginning, it was still entertaining to see how Avon planned out his revenge. The only thing that kind of got on my nerve was Leon's naiveté. Heyer might have exaggerated her innocence and lack of understanding for the entertainment value, but I feel that it went too far. The wa</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7073755410117954227">The book is meant to be a light hearted, entertaining affair. It suited me perfectly for the holiday season. Avon's witty dialogue was always hilarious. Though I guessed the mystery surrounding Leon's birth from the very beginning, it was still entertaining to see how Avon planned out his revenge. The only thing that kind of got on my nerve was Leon's naiveté. Heyer might have exaggerated her innocence and lack of understanding for the entertainment value, but I feel that it went too far. The way she often talked and behaved were really more like a 10 year old instead of a fully grown young woman of 19. Nonetheless, the book as a whole is still a joyful read. <br /><br />3.5 stars</span>

              <a href="/review/list/21428432-tweety?shelf=romance" class="actionLinkLite">romance</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/21428432-tweety?shelf=georgette-heyer" class="actionLinkLite">georgette-heyer</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/21428432-tweety?shelf=grab-during-fire" class="actionLinkLite">grab-during-fire</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/21428432-tweety?shelf=owned" class="actionLinkLite">owned</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer648993912">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7818649158320235597">Léonie, was an imp, she went about calling people Pig-headed and stupid. (Only those who it fitted, of course.) Rupert was Avon younger brother and my! He was a prude! (Yes, yes I know the times!) But he an Léonie were hilarious together, it wouldn't have been the same without him. I completely love this book, I just know my copy will be worn to shreds, especially if I keep laughing so hard. If you want a laugh, Léonie will provide more that enough and Avon's witty combacks are not to be missed.</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7818649158320235597">Léonie, was an imp, she went about calling people Pig-headed and stupid. (Only those who it fitted, of course.) Rupert was Avon younger brother and my! He was a prude! (Yes, yes I know the times!) But he an Léonie were hilarious together, it wouldn't have been the same without him. I completely love this book, I just know my copy will be worn to shreds, especially if I keep laughing so hard. If you want a laugh, Léonie will provide more that enough and Avon's witty combacks are not to be missed. <br /><br />I undertlstand that most people find Avon and Léonie's romance to be icky. Mainly because her called her his child, infant, etc. But, it should be noted that she never viewed him as her father. Perhaps he called her infant as a joke, she was ridiculously funny at times. And, she had an innocent air to her, compared to him she was a child. I feel that Avon called Léonie an <em>enfant </em> to convince himself that he wasn't falling in love with her, it was his way of reminding himself that he didn't have a heart to lose. Which, of course, we all know was utter rot. I think that in the years to come they will fair well as Avon views Léonie as a priceless jewel. He will look after her well and she will show him how to live. <br /><br /> All in all a very funny, sweet story of a rogue page boy, and the infamous Duke of Avon. Avon is the villein of <a title="The Black Moth by Georgette Heyer" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6963840.The_Black_Moth">The Black Moth</a>, I despised him in that book, but in Theses old Shades he has a different name, and he now is ready to change. I loved watching him grow, and turn into a better man. In a way he grew up in this book, he was no longer a scoundrel. Somehow the way he changed was not only amazing, but it was beautiful to watch as well. <br /><br />The only unrealistic thing was that Léonie didn't always act her age, at nineteen she did seem it, especially considering how she was brought up. But, she was a breath of fresh air so I didn't mind too much.<br /><br /> I admit that as far as romantic couples go <a title="Black Sheep by Georgette Heyer" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2857320.Black_Sheep">Black Sheep</a>, <a title="The Convenient Marriage by Georgette Heyer" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6058948.The_Convenient_Marriage">The Convenient Marriage</a>, <a title="Arabella by Georgette Heyer" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6313995.Arabella">Arabella</a>, <a title="Frederica by Georgette Heyer" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/311196.Frederica">Frederica</a> &amp; <a title="Sylvester or the Wicked Uncle by Georgette Heyer" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/601874.Sylvester_or_the_Wicked_Uncle">Sylvester or the Wicked Uncle</a> are better. But this is the funniest! (The Convenient Marriage is possible funnier if you can get passed Horry's stutter) So, I wholeheartedly suggest that all of you go and get a copy right now to read, ignore the H/h's age, and enjoy the adventure. After all, it's a tad like <a title="The Scarlet Pimpernel by Emmuska Orczy" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/431145.The_Scarlet_Pimpernel">The Scarlet Pimpernel</a>. What could be better? <br /><br /><br /> Now I just can't resist adding a quote from one of my favorite passages, I promise to avoid spoilers.<br /><br /><br /><em><strong> (The Duke of Avon in a carriage ride home) </strong> "Versailles does not please you?" <br />"It is so very large,' explained the page.(Léon) 'I feared I had lost you!"<br />"What an alarming thought!" remarked his Grace.<br />"Yes, but you came after all." The deep little voice was getting sleepier and sleepier. "It was all glass and candles, and ladies, and- Bonne Nuit, Monsieur," he sighed. "I am sorry, but everything is muddled, and I am so very tired. I do not think I snore when I sleep, but if I do, then of course you must wake Me. And I might slip, but I hope I shall not. I am right in the corner so perhaps I shall remain here. But if I slip to the floor-"<br /> "Then I suppose I am to pick you up?" said Avon sweetly.<br />"Yes,' agreed Léon, already on the borderland of sleep. 'I won't talk anymore now. Monsieur does not mind?" <br />"Pray do not consider me in the slightest,' answered Avon. "I am here merely to accommodate you. If I disturb you in the slightest I beg you will not hesitate to to mention it. I will then ride on the box." A very sleepy chuckle greeted this sally, and a small hand tucked itself into the Duke's.<br />"I wanted to hold your coat because I thought I should lose you," murmured Léon.<br />"I presume that is why you are holding my hand now?" inquired his Grace. "You are perhaps afraid lest I should hide myself under the seat?" <br />"That is silly,' replied Léon. "Very silly. Bonne nuit, Monsieur."<br />"Bonne nuit mon enfant. You will not lose me - or I you - very easily, I think." There was no answer, but Léon's head sank against his Grace's shoulder, and remained there. <strong> Pg. 63</strong></em><br /><br /><br /><br /><strong><u>PG for the talk of Avon's past indiscretions, mistresses, duels, etc. Swear words were kept to Infidel, imbecile, Idiot, Great Stupid, Pig-headed, and so one. A few D's, H's and, maybe a couple B's, but I don't recall the B's.</u> </strong><br /><br /><br /><strong>Now, wouldn't she make a lovely Léonie? If they ever made These Old Shades into a movie I do think she would do the trick nicely. </strong> <img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1393895667i/8779379.jpg" /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7818649158320235597">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_410010594">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/410010594" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5487874-giedre" class="left imgcol" title="Giedre"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1445608676p2/5487874.jpg" alt="Giedre" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/410010594?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 08, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5487874-giedre" class="user" title="Giedre">Giedre</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/311182.These_Old_Shades" title="Paperback 9780099465829" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer410010594">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7839672691052095567">I'm a fan of romance novels, especially historical, so I tend to read quite a bit of them. I know that Georgette Heyer is sort of a legend in this subgenre, but I have never read any of her books before now. I chose to read <em>These Old Shades</em> because it's part of a trilogy. I thought, hey, I'll read the three books and then decide what I think of her writing. Sadly I didn't enjoy the first installment. At all. <br /><br />I found the Duke of Avon to be a rather fashionable reptile with a fan (yes, you read th</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7839672691052095567">I'm a fan of romance novels, especially historical, so I tend to read quite a bit of them. I know that Georgette Heyer is sort of a legend in this subgenre, but I have never read any of her books before now. I chose to read <em>These Old Shades</em> because it's part of a trilogy. I thought, hey, I'll read the three books and then decide what I think of her writing. Sadly I didn't enjoy the first installment. At all. <br /><br />I found the Duke of Avon to be a rather fashionable reptile with a fan (yes, you read that right). Do not get me wrong, I enjoy these fashionable anti-heroes (see, Eloisa James' the Duke of Villiers), but Heyer's Avon felt rather cardboard. There wasn't much to him, really, apart his vapid need for revenge and calling Leonie "my infant", which made me very, very uncomfortable. <br /><br />Now, Leonie. Apparently she's nineteen. I disagree. I believe that the girl has the emotional and and intellectual maturity of a teenage girl. I'd give her fifteen at most. But that isn't the worst part. That was Leonie's obnoxiousness and tendency to call everyone stupid. She's just extremely annoying and silly. The moment she opened her mouth, I wanted her to shut up. I admit I wanted to strangle her upon occasion. I didn't really see anything deeper inside her personality as Avon did. Leonie's really just an infant. <br /><br />As to the romance between Avon and Leonie, all I can say is that, <em>boy, am I skeeved</em>. This pairing just did not work for me; there just wasn't any chemistry between the protagonists. Also, the age difference just made my skin crawl. And that, believe me, is quite an achievement, because age differences between couples have never fazed me before. I also don't give a damn if it's the man or the woman (if it's hetero romance) who's older. But this time it just felt so wrong. It's not that Avon is twenty-plus years older than Leonie (and a reptile), but rather that she has the maturity of a kid, despite her being nineteen. Honestly, it felt like Avon himself saw her as a child, calling her "my infant" all the time. Even after their unfortunate nuptials. Eww. <br /><br />Soooo... one down, two to go, and then I'll make my decision whether to read more of Heyer's work. But really, I'm hoping that this one was just an odd miss and I'll enjoy the other two in this trilogy.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7839672691052095567">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_676443">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/676443" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/56479-res" class="left imgcol" title="Res"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1177127447p2/56479.jpg" alt="Res" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/676443?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 11, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/56479-res" class="user" title="Res">Res</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/311182.These_Old_Shades" title="Paperback 9780099465829" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/56479-res?shelf=romance" class="actionLinkLite">romance</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer676443">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15273057837807326279">The character of Leonie is immensely likeable (though, like Amanda in Sprig Muslin, she'd make more sense as a 15-year-old than at her stated age, which is 19). I don't quite see in her what Avon says he sees, the results of a life of terrible, constant loneliness.<br /><br />Avon is more problematic. Partly it's a Georgian-culture problem -- it's hard for me to distinguish between an ordinary, irresponsible nobleman of fashion and an actual rake in the old, non-playful sense of the word. We know he kidnapp</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15273057837807326279">The character of Leonie is immensely likeable (though, like Amanda in Sprig Muslin, she'd make more sense as a 15-year-old than at her stated age, which is 19). I don't quite see in her what Avon says he sees, the results of a life of terrible, constant loneliness.<br /><br />Avon is more problematic. Partly it's a Georgian-culture problem -- it's hard for me to distinguish between an ordinary, irresponsible nobleman of fashion and an actual rake in the old, non-playful sense of the word. We know he kidnapped Jenny Merivale as a youth, though evidently he left her marriageable; we know Jenny and her husband won't associate with him, and neither will his sister Fanny's husband, or, we take it, most people of morals, except, for some reason, Hugh Davenant. We know he's nicknamed Satanas; when he declares his love for Leonie, he makes a touching confession of having an unclean heart ... but we never really see or hear reports of specific behaviours that so much set him apart from other men of his set. (Except, I suppose, intentionally provoking a rival to suicide, but this is set up in such a way that you cheer it.)<br /><br />Gender assumptions (Leonie is universally admired for the frankness that comes of having been raised as a boy) and class ones (she's still recognizably a Lady, and the switched baby a peasant, despite their upbringings) are hard to ignore, and as always with gender-switch stories, I feel like the homoeroticism is raised and then hastily shoved offstage again.</span>
